distinctive-feature of phoneme

description::
"distinctive feature
In any language it seems that the sounds used will only differ from each other in a small number of ways. If for example a language had 40 phonemes, then in theory each of those 40 could be utterly different from the other 39. However, in practice there will usually be just a small set of important differences: some of the sounds will be vowels and some consonants; some of the consonants will be plosives and affricates, and the rest will be continuants; some of the continuants will be nasal and some not, and so on. These differences are identified by phonologists, and are known as distinctive features.
There is disagreement about how to define the features (e.g. whether they should be labelled according to articulatory characteristics or acoustic ones), and about how many features are needed in order to be able to classify the sounds of all the languages in the world. See the entry for feature.
[Peter Roach 2009]

"Of course! This is a fascinating topic in linguistics. The frequency of phonemes (the distinct sound units in a language) varies dramatically across the world's languages, but some strong universal patterns emerge.
It's crucial to remember that we're talking about **types of sounds**, not the specific letters of an alphabet. For example, the "th" sound in "think" (the phoneme /Î¸/) is one of the rarest sounds globally, even though the letter combination "th" is common in English.
Hereâ€™s a breakdown of phoneme frequency, from most common to most rare.
### The Most Common and Widespread Phonemes
Almost every language in the world has these sounds. If you were to invent a language, including these would make it sound very natural.
**Vowels: The "Big Five"**
The five-vowel system /i, e, a, o, u/ (as in Spanish or Swahili) is the single most common vowel inventory in the world.
* **/a/** - The open central vowel, as in "f**a**ther." This is arguably the most universal vowel.
* **/i/** - The high front vowel, as in "b**ee**t."
* **/u/** - The high back vowel, as in "b**oo**t."
* **/e/** - The mid-front vowel, as in "b**e**t" or "b**a**te."
* **/o/** - The mid-back vowel, as in "b**o**ught."
**Consonants: The "Top Tier"**
These consonants appear in the vast majority of languages.
* **Stops: /p/, /t/, /k/** - The voiceless stops are extremely common.
* **Nasals: /m/, /n/** - "M" and "N" are virtually universal.
* **The Glide: /j/** - The "y" sound in "**y**es."
* **The Fricative: /s/** - The "s" in "**s**ip."
A language with just these sounds (like Hawaiian, which has 8 consonants and the 5 core vowels) is perfectly functional and occurs naturally.
### Moderately Common Phonemes
These are very widespread but not quite universal. Many major languages feature them.
**Consonants:**
* **Voiced Stops: /b/, /d/, /g/** - While common, they are slightly less frequent than their voiceless counterparts.
* **Other Nasals: /Å‹/** - The "ng" sound in "si**ng**."
* **Fricatives: /h/, /Êƒ/, /f/** - The sounds in "**h**at," "**sh**ip," and "**f**at."
* **Liquid: /l/** - The "l" in "**l**ip."
* **Other: /w/** - The "w" in "**w**et."
### Less Common and Regional Phonemes
These sounds are concentrated in specific language families or regions.
**Consonants:**
* **Uvular Stops: /q/** - The sound in Arabic **Q**ur'an. Common in languages of the Middle East, the Caucasus, and in Inuit languages.
* **Ejectives: /p'/, /t'/, /k'/** - Sounds made with a glottalic airstream mechanism. They are a hallmark of languages in the Caucasus (e.g., Georgian), the Americas (e.g., Quechua, Navajo), and East Africa (e.g., Amharic).
* **Implosives: /É“/, /É—/** - Sounds where the air is sucked inwards. Found in languages of Sub-Saharan Africa (e.g., Swahili has /É“/ and /É—/) and Southeast Asia.
* **Click Consonants: /Ç€/, /Çƒ/, /Ç/** - The famous clicks found exclusively in the Khoisan languages of Southern Africa and as borrowed sounds in some neighboring Bantu languages like Xhosa and Zulu.
### The Rarest Phonemes in the World
These sounds are true linguistic rarities.
**Consonants:**
* **The Voiced Pharyngeal Fricative: /Ê•/** - A guttural sound from deep in the throat. It's a distinctive feature of Arabic, Somali, and some languages of the Caucasus. Very difficult for most non-native speakers to produce.
* **The Alveolar Trill: /r/ (the "rolled R")** - While familiar from Spanish, Italian, and Russian, it is statistically quite rare globally.
* **The Interdental Fricatives: /Î¸/ and /Ã°/** - The "th" sounds in "**th**in" and "**th**is." These are famously rare. They are found in English, Icelandic, Greek, Welsh, and a few dialects of Arabic and Spanishâ€”but almost nowhere else. Many languages substitute these with /t/, /d/, /s/, or /f/.
---
### Key Factors Influencing Phoneme Frequency
1. **Articulatory Ease and Salience:** The most common sounds, like /a/, /i/, /u/, /m/, /p/, /t/, are generally easy to produce and perceive distinctly. They represent a stable, efficient system.
2. **Historical Change and Drift:** Sound changes over time can make common sounds disappear or rare sounds appear. For example, Proto-Indo-European likely had a rolled /r/, but its descendants like English and French lost it.
3. **Geographical and Genetic Links:** Rare sounds often cluster in specific areas due to historical language contact. The "Sprachbund" (linguistic area) of the Caucasus is a prime example, packed with ejectives, uvulars, and pharyngeals.
### Practical Implications
* **Language Learning:** The sounds that are rare globally are often the most difficult for adult learners to acquire. An English speaker struggles with Arabic's /Ê•/, just as a Mandarin speaker struggles with English's /Î¸/.
* **Linguistic Reconstruction:** When reconstructing proto-languages (like Proto-Indo-European), linguists are cautious about proposing phoneme inventories that are highly unusual or contain multiple rare sounds.
* **Conlanging (Constructed Language):** To make a fictional language sound natural, conlangers often start with a core of very common phonemes and then add one or two less common ones for a unique flavor (e.g., Dothraki's use of the uvular stop /q/).
In summary, while every language has a unique sound system, the building blocks are drawn from a shared human inventory where some sounds are fundamental tools and others are exotic specialists."

"That's a fascinating area of linguistic research! Determining the frequency of phonemes across **all** world languages is complex, as the inventory of phonemes varies greatly from language to language, and even the definition of a "phoneme" can be language-specific (see the difference between a phoneme and a phone/allophone).
However, based on typological studies of world languages, we can identify sounds (phones) that are **extremely common** in language inventories, which often correspond to the most frequent phonemes in individual languages.
### ğŸŒŸ Most Common Sounds (Phones) Across Languages
While no single phoneme is technically *universal* (as a phoneme is defined within a specific language's system), the following sounds are nearly ubiquitous in the inventories of world languages:
* **Vowels (The "Basic Three"):**
* $ /a/ $: An open, central or back vowel (like the 'a' in "father"). It is nearly universal for a language to have at least one open vowel, and this sound is one of the most common.
* $ /i/ $: A high, front, unrounded vowel (like the 'ee' in "see").
* $ /u/ $: A high, back, rounded vowel (like the 'oo' in "boot").
* Many languages have only these three vowels, and they represent the maximum possible distance in the vowel space, making them highly distinct.
* **Consonants:**
* **Stops:** The voiceless oral stops are extremely common.
* $ /p/ $: Voiceless bilabial stop (like the 'p' in "pin").
* $ /t/ $: Voiceless alveolar or dental stop (like the 't' in "tin").
* $ /k/ $: Voiceless velar stop (like the 'k' in "kit").
* **Nasals:**
* $ /m/ $: Bilabial nasal (like the 'm' in "man").
* $ /n/ $: Alveolar or dental nasal (like the 'n' in "no").
### ğŸ“Š General Trends in Phoneme Inventories
Cross-linguistic studies, often relying on databases like the World Atlas of Language Structures (WALS) or the UCLA Phonological Segment Inventory Database (UPSID), reveal several general tendencies:
1. **Stops are Universal:** Virtually every known language has at least one plain stop (e.g., a sound like $/t/$ or $/k/$).
2. **Size of Inventories:** The number of phonemes in a language can vary drastically, from as few as 11 (like in Rotokas) to over 100 (like in Archi). The **median** number of phonemes in the world's languages is approximately **33** (with about 22 consonants and 9 distinctive vowels).
3. **Consonant-Heavy Systems:** Most languages have more consonant phonemes than vowel phonemes (typically about two-thirds consonants).
4. **Simpler Sounds are More Common:** Sounds that are articulatorily less complex (like simple stops and common nasals) tend to be more frequent in word usage within a language and more common in the inventories of languages worldwide. Complex sounds (like ejectives, clicks, or multiple contrasting lateral sounds) are much rarer.
5. **Frequency Distribution:** Within any single language, the frequency of phonemes tends to follow a **Zipfian-like distribution**, where a small number of phonemes account for a very large percentage of all sound occurrences.
Would you like to explore the specific phoneme inventory of a particular language, or look for more information on the universal tendencies of vowel or consonant systems?"

"I'll search for information about phoneme frequencies across world languages.This is a fascinating topic! Let me search for more specific information about the most common phonemes across languages.Based on the research, here's what we know about phoneme frequencies across world languages:
## Most Common Phonemes Globally
**The Universal Champion:** The bilabial nasal /m/ is the most frequent phoneme worldwide, appearing in 94.2% of languages (425 out of 451 languages in the UPSID database).
**Most Common Vowels:** The most widespread vowel system consists of five vowels: /i/, /e/, /a/, /o/, /u/. Among individual vowel phonemes, /a/ is the most frequent vowel across languages.
**Most Common Consonants:** The most common consonants are /p/, /t/, /k/, /m/, /n/. More specifically, these consonants occur in more than 50% of all languages in this order: /m/, /k/, /j/, /p/, /w/, /n/, /s/, /t/, /b/, /l/, /h/, /É¡/, /Å‹/, /d/.
## Language Diversity
Languages have between 11 and 141 phonemes on average, with about 22 consonants and 8 vowels per language. Interestingly, over 80% of the 919 different sounds in the UPSID database appear in 10 or fewer languages, showing that most phonemes are quite rare.
## Within-Language Frequency
The frequency of phonemes *within* a language (how often they're used in speech) differs from their cross-linguistic frequency. For example, in English studies, /É™/ (schwa) and /Éª/ were among the most frequently occurring phonemes in actual speech.
The data reveals fascinating patterns about universal tendencies in human language while also highlighting tremendous diversity in sound systems!"
[{2025-11-30 retrieved} https://claude.ai/chat/fd221540-d8ca-4a39-b177-690b8c0b8f75]

phoneSpch.IPA of speech of SpchLago

description::
"The International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA) is an academic standard created by the International Phonetic Association.
IPA is a phonetic notation system that uses a set of symbols to represent each distinct sound that exists in human spoken language. It encompasses all languages spoken on earth. The system was created in 1886 and was last updated in 2005. It consists of 107 letters, 52 diacritics, and four prosodic marks."
[http://www.internationalphoneticalphabet.org/]

[https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/IPA_chart_2020.svg]

number of IPA

description::
Ã— Mcsh-creation: {2025-11-15},
"IPA numbers are a legacy system of coding the symbols of the International Phonetic Alphabet. They were the organizational basis for XSAMPA and the IPA Extensions block of Unicode.
Following the Kiel Convention in 1989, most letters, diacritics and other symbols of the IPA were assigned a 3-digit numerical code, with updates through 2005. The purpose was to identify IPA symbols explicitly in an era of competing computer encodings, and thus to prevent confusion between similar characters (such as âŸ¨ÉµâŸ© and âŸ¨Î¸âŸ©, âŸ¨É¤âŸ© and âŸ¨É£âŸ©, âŸ¨ÊƒâŸ© and âŸ¨Ê„âŸ©, âŸ¨É«âŸ© and âŸ¨É¬âŸ© or âŸ¨ÇâŸ© and âŸ¨â€–âŸ©) in such situations as the printing of manuscripts. The system never saw much if any use and is now defunct, having been superseded by Unicode.[1]
The semantic and graphic categories of the symbols are assigned different ranges of numbers: The 100 series are IPA consonants, the 200s retired and non-IPA consonants, the 300s vowels, the 400s diacritics, the 500s suprasegmentals, the 600s extIPA, the 700s capital letters and the 900s delimiters.[1] Some symbols have more than one code.[2]"
[{2025-11-15 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPA_number]

## 1. The â€œUnmarkedâ€ Core: Fiveâ€‘Vowel System

## 2. Expansion: Splits, Length, and Centralization

1. **Length distinctions**
* *e* â†’ /e/ vs. /eË/ (e.g. Classical Greek, early Old English)

## 5. Areal and Typological Patterns
* **Tonal vs. Nonâ€‘tonal correlates:** Tone languages often have fewer vowel qualities but richer tone contrasts (e.g. Mandarin: 5 vowels + 4 tones).
* **Vowel harmony zones:** Uralic, Altaic, and many African families favor harmonic splits (e.g. front vs. back series).
* **Island vs. continental:** Isolated languages (e.g. Pacific islands) often preserve small inventories; contactâ€‘rich areas (e.g. Europe) favor expansions and borrowings.

* **Perceptual distinctiveness:** Undershot targets in crowded vowel spaces â†’ chain shifts to maintain contrast.

"The evolution of vowel phonemes is a complex process shaped by historical, linguistic, and social factors. Vowel phonemes, the distinct vowel sounds in a language, evolve through changes in pronunciation, phonemic mergers, splits, and shifts influenced by phonetic environments, language contact, and internal linguistic dynamics. Below is a concise overview of how vowel phonemes evolve, with examples grounded in linguistic history.
### 1. **Phonetic Changes and Sound Shifts**
Vowel phonemes often change through systematic shifts in articulation, such as tongue position (height, frontness/backness) or lip rounding. These shifts can affect entire vowel systems.
- **Great Vowel Shift (English, 14thâ€“17th centuries)**: In Middle English, long vowels underwent a dramatic shift. For example, /iË/ in *bite* shifted from [iË] to [aÉª], and /uË/ in *house* from [uË] to [aÊŠ]. This chain shift raised high vowels and diphthongized mid vowels, reshaping the English vowel system.
- **Chain Shifts**: Vowels often shift in coordinated patterns. In a *push chain*, a vowel moves into the space of another, pushing it elsewhere (e.g., in some Germanic languages, /a/ â†’ /Ã¦/ pushed /Ã¦/ â†’ /e/). In a *pull chain*, a vowel vacates a space, allowing others to fill it.
### 2. **Mergers and Splits**
Vowel phonemes can merge (two sounds become one) or split (one sound diverges into two), altering the phonemic inventory.
- **Mergers**: In some American English dialects, the *cot-caught merger* collapsed /É‘/ (as in *cot*) and /É”/ (as in *caught*) into a single phoneme, reducing the vowel inventory. This is common in Western U.S. dialects.
- **Splits**: The *bad-lad split* in Australian English distinguishes /Ã¦/ in *bad* (lengthened to [Ã¦Ë]) from /Ã¦/ in *lad* ([Ã¦]). Splits often arise from phonetic conditioning, like vowel length or adjacent consonants.
### 3. **Phonetic Conditioning and Allophonic Variation**
Vowel phonemes evolve when allophones (variant pronunciations) become phonemic due to changes in context.
- **Umlaut**: In Germanic languages, vowels shifted due to influence from neighboring sounds. For example, in Old English, *mus* (â€œmouseâ€) became *mys* (modern *mice*) because a plural suffix *-i* fronted the vowel /u/ to /y/. Over time, this created new phonemes.
- **Vowel Harmony**: In languages like Turkish or Hungarian, vowels harmonize in features like frontness or rounding, which can lead to new phonemic distinctions as harmony rules change or weaken.
### 4. **Language Contact and Borrowing**
Contact with other languages can introduce or modify vowel phonemes.
- **Norman Influence on English**: After the Norman Conquest (1066), French loanwords introduced nasal vowels (e.g., in *vin* â€œwineâ€) to Middle English, though these later merged with oral vowels.
- **Colonial Influence**: In languages like Hindi, contact with English has led to the adoption of new vowel distinctions in loanwords, though these are often adapted to fit native phonology.
### 5. **Vowel Reduction and Loss**
Vowels often weaken or disappear in unstressed syllables, reshaping phoneme systems.
- **Schwa Development**: In many languages, unstressed vowels reduce to /É™/ (schwa). In English, this is evident in words like *about* ([É™ËˆbaÊŠt]). Over time, reduction can eliminate phonemic distinctions in unstressed positions.
- **Vowel Elision**: In some Romance languages, like French, unstressed vowels were lost entirely, simplifying the vowel system compared to Latin.
### 6. **Typological Patterns**
Languages tend to maintain balanced vowel systems, often with 5â€“7 vowels (e.g., /i, e, a, o, u/), as seen in Spanish or Japanese. Systems with more or fewer vowels evolve through mergers, splits, or shifts to maintain symmetry and contrast.
- **Small Inventories**: Languages like Arabic (3 vowels: /i, a, u/) maintain minimal systems, but length or quality distinctions add complexity.
- **Large Inventories**: Languages like Danish or French have complex vowel systems with nasal, rounded, or front/back distinctions, often from historical splits or borrowings.
### 7. **Social and Regional Factors**
Vowel phonemes evolve differently across dialects due to social factors like migration or prestige forms.
- **Northern Cities Vowel Shift**: In U.S. cities like Chicago, vowels like /Ã¦/ (as in *cat*) raised to [É›É™], while /É‘/ and /É”/ shifted, driven by urban social dynamics.
- **Standardization**: Prestige dialects, like Received Pronunciation in English, can stabilize certain vowel phonemes while marginalizing others.
### Examples Across Language Families
- **Indo-European**: Proto-Indo-European had a simple vowel system (/e, o, a/) that expanded in daughter languages like Latin or Greek through diphthongization and lengthening.
- **Bantu Languages**: Vowel systems often reduced from 7 to 5 vowels due to mergers, as in Swahili.
- **Sino-Tibetan**: Mandarin Chinese developed tone-vowel interactions, where pitch contours became phonemic, influencing vowel perception.
### Sources and Methodology

VowlPhnm./a/-(26-central-open)

description::
"The open front unrounded vowel, or low front unrounded vowel,[1] is a type of vowel sound, used in some spoken languages. It is one of the eight primary cardinal vowels, not directly intended to correspond to a vowel sound of a specific language but rather to serve as a fundamental reference point in a phonetic measuring system.[2]
The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA) that represents this sound is âŸ¨aâŸ©, and in the IPA vowel chart it is positioned at the lower-left corner. However, the accuracy of the quadrilateral vowel chart is disputed, and the sound has been analyzed acoustically as extra-open at a position where the front/back distinction has lost its significance. There are also differing interpretations of the exact quality of the vowel: the classic sound recording of [a] by Daniel Jones is slightly more front but not quite as open as that by John Wells.[3]
In practice, it is considered normal by many phoneticians to use the symbol âŸ¨aâŸ© for an open central unrounded vowel and instead approximate the open front unrounded vowel with âŸ¨Ã¦âŸ© (which officially signifies a near-open front unrounded vowel).[4] This is the usual practice, for example, in the historical study of the English language. The loss of separate symbols for open and near-open front vowels is usually considered unproblematic, because the perceptual difference between the two is quite small, and very few languages contrast the two. If there is a need to specify the backness of the vowel as fully front one can use the symbol âŸ¨Ã¦ÌâŸ©, which denotes a lowered near-open front unrounded vowel.
The Hamont dialect of Limburgish has been reported to contrast long open front, central and back unrounded vowels.[5] This is extremely unusual."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel]

VowlPhnm./i/-(14-front-closed)

description::
"The close front unrounded vowel, or high front unrounded vowel,[1] is a type of vowel sound that occurs in most spoken languages, represented in the International Phonetic Alphabet by the symbol i. It is similar to the vowel sound in the English word meetâ€”and often called long-e in American English.[2] Although in English this sound has additional length (usually being represented as /iË/) and is not normally pronounced as a pure vowel (it is a slight diphthong), some dialects have been reported to pronounce the phoneme as a pure sound.[3] A pure [i] sound is also heard in many other languages, such as French, in words like chic.
The close front unrounded vowel is the vocalic equivalent of the palatal approximant [j]. The two are almost identical featurally. They alternate with each other in certain languages, such as French, and in the diphthongs of some languages, [iÌ¯] with the non-syllabic diacritic and [j] are used in different transcription systems to represent the same sound.
Languages that use the Latin script commonly use the letter âŸ¨iâŸ© to represent this sound, though there are some exceptions: in English orthography that letter is usually associated with /aÉª/ (as in bite) or /Éª/ (as in bit), and /iË/ is more commonly represented by âŸ¨eâŸ©, âŸ¨eaâŸ©, âŸ¨eeâŸ©, âŸ¨ieâŸ© or âŸ¨eiâŸ©, as in the words scene, bean, meet, niece, conceive; (see Great Vowel Shift). Irish orthography reflects both etymology and whether preceding consonants are broad or slender, so such combinations as âŸ¨aÃ­âŸ©, âŸ¨eiâŸ©, and âŸ¨aÃ­oâŸ© all represent /iË/."

VowlPhnm./u/-(34-back-close)

description::
"The close back rounded vowel, or high back rounded vowel,[1] is a type of vowel sound used in many spoken languages. The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents this sound is âŸ¨uâŸ©, and the equivalent X-SAMPA symbol is u.
In most languages, this rounded vowel is pronounced with protruded lips ('endolabial'). However, in a few cases the lips are compressed ('exolabial').
The close back rounded vowel is almost identical featurally to the labio-velar approximant [w]. [u] alternates with [w] in certain languages, such as French, and in the diphthongs of some languages, [uÌ¯] with the non-syllabic diacritic and [w] are used in different transcription systems to represent the same sound."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel]

====== lagoGreek:
* McsElln.ÏƒÏÎ¼Ï†Ï‰Î½Î¿Î ÏÏ†Îº!Ï„Î¿!=ConsPhnm,

airstream of ConsPhnm

description::
* pulmonic,
* pulmonicNo,

"Pulmonic Consonants: The Breath-Powered Sounds of Speech
Pulmonic consonants are the foundational sounds of most of the world's languages, produced by the outward flow of air from the lungs. The term "pulmonic" itself is derived from the Latin word pulmo, meaning lung. These consonants are formed when the stream of air, or the airstream, is initiated by the lungs and then obstructed or modified at some point in the vocal tract.
The vast majority of consonants in English and other major world languages are pulmonic. In fact, many languages exclusively use pulmonic consonants for their entire consonant inventory. These sounds are contrasted with non-pulmonic consonants, which utilize different airstream mechanisms, such as the clicks found in some African languages, ejectives, and implosives.
The production of a pulmonic consonant involves a complex interplay of several articulatory features:
Airstream Mechanism: The process begins with the lungs pushing air out, a process known as a pulmonic egressive airstream.
Place of Articulation: This refers to the point in the vocal tract where the airflow is constricted. This can range from the lips (bilabial sounds like /p/ and /b/) to the back of the throat (glottal sounds like /h/). Other places of articulation include the teeth (dental), the alveolar ridge just behind the teeth (alveolar), the hard palate (palatal), and the soft palate or velum (velar).
Manner of Articulation: This describes how the airflow is modified. It can be completely stopped and then released (plosives or stops like /t/ and /d/), forced through a narrow channel (fricatives like /f/ and /s/), or diverted through the nasal cavity (nasals like /m/ and /n/). Other manners include approximants, laterals, and affricates.
Phonation: This refers to the state of the vocal cords during the articulation of the sound. If the vocal cords vibrate, the sound is "voiced" (e.g., /b/, /d/, /g/, /z/). If they do not vibrate, the sound is "voiceless" (e.g., /p/, /t/, /k/, /s/).
The International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA) provides a comprehensive chart of all known pulmonic consonants, systematically organizing them by their place and manner of articulation, as well as their voicing. This chart is an essential tool for linguists, phoneticians, and speech-language pathologists in analyzing and transcribing the sounds of human language."
[{2025-07-27 retrieved} https://gemini.google.com/app/cf5725c64ecc5d49]

name::
* McsEngl.ConsPhnm'airstream,

place-of-articulation of ConsPhnm

description::
"Places of articulation (active and passive)
1. Exo-labial (outer part of lip)
2. Endo-labial (inner part of lip)
3. Dental (teeth)
4. Alveolar (front part of alveolar ridge)
5. Post-alveolar (rear part of alveolar ridge & slightly behind it)
6. Pre-palatal (front part of hard palate that arches upward)
7. Palatal (hard palate)
8. Velar (soft palate)
9. Uvular (a.k.a. Post-velar; uvula)
10. Pharyngeal (pharyngeal wall)
11. Glottal (a.k.a. Laryngeal; vocal folds)
12. Epiglottal (epiglottis)
13. Radical (tongue root)
14. Postero-dorsal (back of tongue body)
15. Antero-dorsal (front of tongue body)
16. Laminal (tongue blade)
17. Apical (apex or tongue tip)
18. Sub-laminal (a.k.a. Sub-apical; underside of tongue)
Notes:
- Some phoneticians may define terms slightly differently and may include more or less distinctions (e.g. Post-velar could be an area between Velar and Uvular; or Exo-labial and Endo-labial could be subsumed under Labial). The scheme used above is from Catford (1977).
- Pharyngeal can usefully be divided into different regions.
- The term Retroflex is better thought of as a particular combination of a sublaminal/apical active articulator and a alveolar/postalveolar/prepalatal/palatal passive region.
- Radical is often considered to be both the upper tongue root (#13) and the lower tongue root including the epiglottis (#12)."

"Aspiration in the Chinese language refers to the puff of air that accompanies the pronunciation of certain consonants, particularly the voiceless stops p, t, k, c, and ch. This aspiration is a key feature in distinguishing between different sounds and words in Chinese.
For example, the word "pÄ" (to climb) is aspirated, while "bÄ" (eight) is not. This difference in aspiration is crucial for understanding the meaning of these words.
Here are some resources where you can learn more about aspiration in Chinese:
* **Chinese Aspirated Sounds: Basics & Examples | Vaia:** [https://www.vaia.com/en-us/explanations/chinese/chinese-grammar/chinese-aspirated-sounds/](https://www.vaia.com/en-us/explanations/chinese/chinese-grammar/chinese-aspirated-sounds/)
* **Cambridge Dictionary - Aspiration:** [https://dictionary.cambridge.org/dictionary/english-chinese-simplified/aspiration](https://dictionary.cambridge.org/dictionary/english-chinese-simplified/aspiration)
If you have any other questions, feel free to ask."

ConsPhnm.labial

description::
"Labial consonants are consonants in which one or both lips are the active articulator. The two common labial articulations are bilabials, articulated using both lips, and labiodentals, articulated with the lower lip against the upper teeth, both of which are present in English. A third labial articulation is dentolabials, articulated with the upper lip against the lower teeth (the reverse of labiodental), normally only found in pathological speech. Generally precluded are linguolabials, in which the tip of the tongue contacts the posterior side of the upper lip, making them coronals, though sometimes, they behave as labial consonants.[clarification needed]
The most common distribution between bilabials and labiodentals is the English one, in which the nasal and the stops, [m], [p], and [b], are bilabial and the fricatives, [f], and [v], are labiodental. The voiceless bilabial fricative, voiced bilabial fricative, and the bilabial approximant do not exist as the primary realizations of any sounds in English, but they occur in many languages. For example, the Spanish consonant written b or v is pronounced, between vowels, as a voiced bilabial approximant.
Lip rounding, or labialization, is a common approximant-like co-articulatory feature. English /w/ is a voiced labialized velar approximant, which is far more common than the purely labial approximant [Î²Ì]. In the languages of the Caucasus, labialized dorsals like /kÊ·/ and /qÊ·/ are very common.
Very few languages, however, make a distinction purely between bilabials and labiodentals, making "labial" usually a sufficient specification of a language's phonemes. One exception is Ewe, which has both kinds of fricatives, but the labiodentals are produced with greater articulatory force."
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labial_consonant]

ConsPhnm.coronal

description::
"Coronals are consonants articulated with the flexible front part of the tongue. Among places of articulation, only the coronal consonants can be divided into as many articulation types: apical (using the tip of the tongue), laminal (using the blade of the tongue), domed (with the tongue bunched up), or subapical (using the underside of the tongue) as well as different postalveolar articulations (some of which also involve the back of the tongue as an articulator): palato-alveolar, alveolo-palatal and retroflex. Only the front of the tongue (coronal) has such dexterity among the major places of articulation, allowing such variety of distinctions. Coronals have another dimension, grooved, to make sibilants in combination with the orientations above."
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronal_consonant]

name::
* McsEngl.ConsPhnm.coronal,
* McsEngl.phoneme.coronal,
* McsEngl.coronal,
* McsEngl.coronal-ConsPhnm,

ConsPhnm.dorsal

description::
"Dorsal consonants are consonants articulated with the back of the tongue (the dorsum). They include the palatal, velar and, in some cases, alveolo-palatal and uvular consonants. They contrast with coronal consonants, articulated with the flexible front of the tongue, and laryngeal consonants, articulated in the pharyngeal cavity."
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dorsal_consonant]

ConsPhnm.larygeal

description::
Â· pharyngeal denoted with 'f': /kf/ /hf/ ... {2025-04-23}
"Laryngeal consonants (a term often used interchangeably with guttural consonants) are consonants with their primary articulation in the larynx. The laryngeal consonants comprise the pharyngeal consonants (including the epiglottals), the glottal consonants,[1][2] and for some languages uvular consonants.[3]
The term laryngeal is often taken to be synonymous with glottal, but the larynx consists of more than just the glottis (vocal folds): it also includes the epiglottis and aryepiglottic folds. In a broad sense, therefore, laryngeal articulations include the radical consonants, which involve the root of the tongue. The diversity of sounds produced in the larynx is the subject of ongoing research, and the terminology is evolving.
The term laryngeal consonant is also used for laryngealized consonants articulated in the upper vocal tract, such as Arabic 'emphatics' and Korean 'tense' consonants."
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laryngeal_consonant]

ConsPhnm.apical

description::
"An apical consonant is a phone (speech sound) produced by obstructing the air passage with the tip of the tongue (apex) in conjunction with upper articulators from lips to postalveolar, and possibly prepalatal.[1][2] It contrasts with laminal consonants, which are produced by creating an obstruction with the blade of the tongue, just behind the tip. Sometimes apical is used exclusively for an articulation that involves only the tip of the tongue and apicolaminal for an articulation that involves both the tip and the blade of the tongue.[3] However, the distinction is not always made and the latter one may be called simply apical, especially when describing an apical dental articulation.[1][4] As there is some laminal contact in the alveolar region, the apicolaminal dental consonants are also labelled as denti-alveolar.
It is not a very common distinction and is typically applied only to fricatives and affricates. Thus, many varieties of English have either apical or laminal pairs of [t]/[d]. However, some varieties of Arabic, including Hadhrami Arabic in Yemen, realize [t] as laminal but [d] as apical.
Basque uses the distinction for alveolar fricatives, as does Serbo-Croatian. Mandarin Chinese uses it for postalveolar fricatives (the "alveolo-palatal" and "retroflex" series). Lillooet uses it as a secondary feature in contrasting velarized and non-velarized affricates. A distinction between apical and laminal is common in Australian Aboriginal languages for nasals, plosives and (usually) lateral approximants.
Most dialects in the Bengaliâ€“Assamese continuum distinguish between dentalâ€“laminal alveolar stops and apical alveolar stops. In Upper Assamese, they have merged and leave only the apical alveolar stops. In Western Bengali apical alveolars are replaced by apical post-alveolars.
In the International Phonetic Alphabet, the diacritic for apical consonants is U+033A â—ŒÌº COMBINING INVERTED BRIDGE BELOW."
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apical_consonant]

ConsPhnm.oral

description::
"An oral consonant is a consonant sound in speech that is made by allowing air to escape from the mouth, as opposed to the nose, as in a nasal consonant. To create an intended oral consonant sound, the entire mouth plays a role in modifying the air's passageway. This rapid modification of the air passageway using the tongue and lips makes changes to the waveform of the sound by compressing and expanding the air. In addition to the nose and mouth, the vocal cords and lungs also make a contribution to producing speech by controlling the volume (amplitude) and pitch (frequency) of the sound. The use of the vocal cords will also determine whether the consonant is voiced or voiceless. The vast majority of consonants are oral, such as, for example [p], [w], [v] and [x]. The others are nasal, such as the nasal occlusives [m] or [É²].
Before there appeared the consonantal opposition nasal/oral, consonant was distinguished from vowel as closed tract from open tract. Once the nasal consonant has been opposed to the oral as presence to absence of the open tract, the contrast consonant/vowel is revalued as presence vs. absence of a closed tract.[1]"
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oral_consonant]

* McsEngl.ConsPhnm.oral,
* McsEngl.phoneme.oral,
* McsEngl.oral-ConsPhnm,

* McsElln.ÏƒÏ„Î¿Î¼Î±Ï„Î¹ÎºÏŒ-ÏƒÏÎ¼Ï†Ï‰Î½Î¿Î ÏÏ†Îº!=oral,

ConsPhnm.nasal

description::
"In phonetics, a nasal, also called a nasal occlusive or nasal stop in contrast with an oral stop or nasalized consonant, is an occlusive consonant produced with a lowered velum, allowing air to escape freely through the nose. The vast majority of consonants are oral consonants. Examples of nasals in English are [n], [Å‹] and [m], in words such as nose, bring and mouth. Nasal occlusives are nearly universal in human languages. There are also other kinds of nasal consonants in some languages."
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nasal_consonant]

ConsPhnm.stop

description::
"In phonetics, a stop, also known as a plosive or oral occlusive, is a consonant in which the vocal tract is blocked so that all airflow ceases.
The occlusion may be made with the tongue blade ([t], [d]) tongue body ([k], [É¡]), lips ([p], [b]), or glottis ([Ê”]). Stops contrast with nasals, where the vocal tract is blocked but airflow continues through the nose, as in /m/ and /n/, and with fricatives, where partial occlusion impedes but does not block airflow in the vocal tract."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stop_consonant]

ConsPhnm.approximant

description::
"Approximants are speech sounds that involve the articulators approaching each other but not narrowly enough nor with enough articulatory precision to create turbulent airflow. Therefore, approximants fall between fricatives, which do produce a turbulent airstream, and vowels, which produce no turbulence. This class is composed of sounds like [É¹] (as in rest) and semivowels like [j] and [w] (as in yes and west, respectively), as well as lateral approximants like [l] (as in less)."
[{2019-08-10} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Approximant_consonant]

ConsPhnm.affricat

description::
"An affricate is a consonant that begins as a stop and releases as a fricative, generally with the same place of articulation (most often coronal). It is often difficult to decide if a stop and fricative form a single phoneme or a consonant pair. English has two affricate phonemes, /tÍ¡Êƒ/ and /dÍ¡Ê’/, often spelled ch and j, respectively."
[{2019-08-10} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Affricate_consonant]

* McsEngl.affricat-consonant--of-SpchLago,
* McsEngl.ConsPhnm.affricat,
* McsEngl.phoneme.affricat,

ConsPhnm.fricative

description::
"Fricatives are consonants produced by forcing air through a narrow channel made by placing two articulators close together. These may be the lower lip against the upper teeth, in the case of [f]; the back of the tongue against the soft palate, in the case of German [x] (the final consonant of Bach); or the side of the tongue against the molars, in the case of Welsh [É¬] (appearing twice in the name Llanelli). This turbulent airflow is called frication.
A particular subset of fricatives are the sibilants. When forming a sibilant, one still is forcing air through a narrow channel, but in addition, the tongue is curled lengthwise to direct the air over the edge of the teeth[1]. English [s], [z], [Êƒ], and [Ê’] are examples of sibilants.
The usage of two other terms is less standardized: "Spirant" is an older term for fricatives used by some American and European phoneticians and phonologists. "Strident" could mean just "sibilant", but some authors[who?] include also labiodental and uvular fricatives in the class."
[{2019-08-10} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fricative_consonant]

ConsPhnm.sibilant

description::
"Sibilants are fricative consonants of higher amplitude and pitch, made by directing a stream of air with the tongue towards the teeth.[1] Examples of sibilants are the consonants at the beginning of the English words sip, zip, ship, and genre. The symbols in the International Phonetic Alphabet used to denote the sibilant sounds in these words are, respectively, [s] [z] [Êƒ] [Ê’]. Sibilants have a characteristically intense sound, which accounts for their paralinguistic use in getting one's attention (e.g. calling someone using "psst!" or quieting someone using "shhhh!").
In the alveolar hissing sibilants [s] and [z], the back of the tongue forms a narrow channel (is grooved) to focus the stream of air more intensely, resulting in a high pitch. With the hushing sibilants (occasionally termed shibilants), such as English [Êƒ], [tÊƒ], [Ê’], and [dÊ’], the tongue is flatter, and the resulting pitch lower.[2][3]
A broader category is stridents, which include more fricatives than sibilants such as uvulars. Because all sibilants are also stridents, the terms are sometimes used interchangeably. However, the terms do not mean the same thing. The English stridents are /f, v, s, z, Êƒ, Ê’, tÊƒ, dÊ’/. Sibilants are a higher pitched subset of the stridents. The English sibilants are /s, z, Êƒ, Ê’, tÊƒ, dÊ’/. On the other hand, /f/ and /v/ are stridents, but not sibilants, because they are lower in pitch.[4][5][6]
"Stridency" refers to the perceptual intensity of the sound of a sibilant consonant, or obstacle fricatives or affricates, which refers to the critical role of the teeth in producing the sound as an obstacle to the airstream. Non-sibilant fricatives and affricates produce their characteristic sound directly with the tongue or lips etc. and the place of contact in the mouth, without secondary involvement of the teeth.[citation needed]
The characteristic intensity of sibilants means that small variations in tongue shape and position are perceivable, with the result that there are many sibilant types that contrast in various languages."
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sibilant]

* McsEngl.ConsPhnm.sibilant,
* McsEngl.phoneme.sibilant,
* McsEngl.sibilant-ConsPhnm,

ConsPhnm.sonorant

description::
"In phonetics and phonology, a sonorant or resonant is a speech sound that is produced with continuous, non-turbulent airflow in the vocal tract; these are the manners of articulation that are most often voiced in the world's languages. Vowels are sonorants, as are nasals like [m] and [n], liquids like [l] and [r], and semivowels like [j] and [w]. This set of sounds contrasts with the obstruents (stops, affricates and fricatives).[1]
For some authors, only the term resonant is used with this broader meaning, while sonorant is restricted to consonants, referring to nasals and liquids but not vocoids (vowels and semivowels).[2]"
[{2022-08-28 retrieved} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sonorant]

* McsEngl.ConsPhnm.sonorant,

ConsPhnm.brathy

description::
Ã— McsHitp-creation: {2025-05-03},
"Breathy voice (also called murmured voice, whispery voice, soughing and susurration) is a phonation in which the vocal folds vibrate, as they do in normal (modal) voicing, but are adjusted to let more air escape[1] which produces a sighing-like sound. A simple breathy phonation, [É¦] (not actually a fricative consonant, as a literal reading of the IPA chart would suggest), can sometimes be heard as an allophone of English /h/ between vowels, such as in the word behind, for some speakers."

ConsPhnm./v/

description::
Â· example: vine,
"The voiced labiodental fricative is a type of consonantal sound used in some spoken languages. The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents this sound is âŸ¨vâŸ©, and the equivalent X-SAMPA symbol is v.
The sound is similar to voiced alveolar fricative /z/ in that it is familiar to most European speakers, but cross-linguistically it is a fairly uncommon sound, being only a quarter as frequent as [w]. Moreover, Most languages that have /z/ also have /v/ and similarly to /z/, the overwhelming majority of languages with [v] are languages of Europe, Africa, or Western Asia, although the similar labiodental approximant /Ê‹/ is also common in India. The presence of [v] and absence of [w], is a very distinctive areal feature of European languages and those of adjacent areas of Siberia and Central Asia. Speakers of East Asian languages that lack this sound tend to pronounce it as [b] (Korean and Japanese), or [f]/[w] (Cantonese and Mandarin), thus failing to distinguish a number of English minimal pairs.[citation needed]
In certain languages, such as Danish, Faroese, Icelandic or Norwegian the voiced labiodental fricative is in a free variation with the labiodental approximant."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_fricative]

ConsPhnm./th/-[Î¸]

description::
Â· example: thanks,
Â· Greek: Î¸,
Â· char.952'Î¸'
"The voiceless dental non-sibilant fricative is a type of consonantal sound used in some spoken languages. It is familiar to English speakers as the 'th' in thing. Though rather rare as a phoneme in the world's inventory of languages, it is encountered in some of the most widespread and influential (see below). The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents this sound is âŸ¨Î¸âŸ©, and the equivalent X-SAMPA symbol is T. The IPA symbol is the Greek letter theta, which is used for this sound in post-classical Greek, and the sound is thus often referred to as "theta".
The dental non-sibilant fricatives are often called "interdental" because they are often produced with the tongue between the upper and lower teeth, and not just against the back of the upper or lower teeth, as they are with other dental consonants.
This sound and its voiced counterpart are rare phonemes occurring in 4% of languages in a phonological analysis of 2155 languages. Among the more than 60 languages with over 10 million speakers, only English, various dialects of Arabic, Standard European Spanish, Swahili (in words derived from Arabic), Burmese, and Greek have the voiceless dental non-sibilant fricative.[citation needed] Speakers of languages and dialects without the sound sometimes have difficulty producing or distinguishing it from similar sounds, especially if they have had no chance to acquire it in childhood, and typically replace it with a voiceless alveolar fricative (/s/) (as in Indonesian), voiceless dental stop (/t/), or a voiceless labiodental fricative (/f/); known respectively as th-alveolarization, th-stopping, and th-fronting.
The sound is known to have disappeared from a number of languages, e.g. from most of the Germanic languages or dialects, where it is retained only in Scots, English, Elfdalian, and Icelandic, but it is alveolar in the last of these. Among non-Germanic Indo-European languages as a whole, the sound was also once much more widespread, but is today preserved in a few languages including the Brythonic languages, Castilian Spanish, Venetian, Albanian, few Occitan dialects and Greek. It has likewise disappeared from many Semitic languages, such as Hebrew (excluding Yemenite Hebrew) and many modern varieties of Arabic (excluding Tunisian, Mesopotamian Arabic and various dialects in the Arabian Peninsula which still include it)."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative]

ConsPhnm./dh/-[Ã°]

description::
Â· example: this,
Â· Greek: Î´,
Â· char.948'Î´' char.240'Ã°'
"The voiced dental fricative is a consonant sound used in some spoken languages. It is familiar to English-speakers, as the th sound in father. Its symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet is eth, or [Ã°] and was taken from the Old English and Icelandic letter eth, which could stand for either a voiced or unvoiced interdental non-sibilant fricative.
The letter âŸ¨Ã°âŸ© is sometimes used to represent the dental approximant, a similar sound, which no language is known to contrast with a dental non-sibilant fricative,[1] but the approximant is more clearly written with the lowering diacritic: âŸ¨Ã°ÌâŸ©. Very rarely used variant transcriptions of the dental approximant include âŸ¨Ê‹Ì âŸ© (retracted [Ê‹]), âŸ¨É¹ÌŸâŸ© (advanced [É¹]) and âŸ¨É¹ÌªâŸ© (dentalized [É¹]). It has been proposed that either a turned âŸ¨Ã°âŸ© or reversed âŸ¨Ã°âŸ© be used as a dedicated symbol for the dental approximant, but despite occasional usage this has not gained general acceptance. Dental non-sibilant fricatives are often called "interdental" because they are often produced with the tongue between the upper and lower teeth (as in English), and not just against the back of the upper teeth, as they are with other dental consonants.
This sound and its unvoiced counterpart are rare phonemes. Almost all languages of Europe and Asia, such as German, French, Persian, Japanese, and Mandarin, lack the sound. Native speakers of languages without the sound often have difficulty enunciating or distinguishing it, and they replace it with a voiced alveolar sibilant [z], a voiced dental stop or voiced alveolar stop [d], or a voiced labiodental fricative [v]; known respectively as th-alveolarization, th-stopping, and th-fronting. As for Europe, there seems to be a great arc where the sound (and/or its unvoiced variant) is present. Most of Mainland Europe lacks the sound. However, some "periphery" languages as Gascon, Welsh, English, Icelandic, Elfdalian, Kven, Northern Sami, Inari Sami, Skolt Sami, Ume Sami, Mari, Greek, Albanian, Sardinian, some dialects of Basque and most speakers of Spanish have the sound in their consonant inventories, as phonemes or allophones.
Within Turkic languages, Bashkir and Turkmen have both voiced and voiceless dental non-sibilant fricatives among their consonants. Among Semitic languages, they are used in Turoyo, Modern Standard Arabic, albeit not by all speakers of modern Arabic dialects, as well as in some dialects of Hebrew and Assyrian Neo-Aramaic."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voiced_dental_fricative]

ConsPhnm./t/

description::
Â· example: tip,
Â· Greek: Ï„,
Â· pinyin: d,
"The voiceless alveolar stop is a type of consonantal sound used in almost all spoken languages. The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents voiceless dental, alveolar, and postalveolar stops is âŸ¨tâŸ©, and the equivalent X-SAMPA symbol is t. The dental stop can be distinguished with the underbridge diacritic, âŸ¨tÌªâŸ©, the postalveolar with a retraction line, âŸ¨tÌ âŸ©, and the Extensions to the IPA have a double underline diacritic which can be used to explicitly specify an alveolar pronunciation, âŸ¨tÍ‡âŸ©.
The [t] sound is a very common sound cross-linguistically; the most common consonant phonemes of the world's languages are [t], [k] and [p]. Most languages have at least a plain [t], and some distinguish more than one variety. Some languages without a [t] are Hawaiian (except for NiÊ»ihau; Hawaiian uses a voiceless velar stop [k] for loanwords with [t]), colloquial Samoan (which also lacks an [n]), Abau, and NÇng of South Africa."

ConsPhnm./s/

description::
Â· example: sit,
Â· Greek: Ïƒ,Ï‚,
"The voiceless alveolar sibilant is a common consonant sound in vocal languages. It is the sound in English words such as sea and pass, and is represented in the International Phonetic Alphabet with âŸ¨sâŸ©. It has a characteristic high-pitched, highly perceptible hissing sound. For this reason, it is often used to get someone's attention, using a call often written as sssst! or psssst!.
The voiceless alveolar sibilant [s] is one of the most common sounds cross-linguistically. If a language has fricatives, it will most likely have [s].[2] However, some languages have a related sibilant sound, such as [Êƒ], but no [s]. In addition, sibilants are absent from Australian Aboriginal languages, in which fricatives are rare; even the few indigenous Australian languages that have developed fricatives do not have sibilants.[citation needed]
The voiceless alveolar retracted sibilant (commonly termed the voiceless apico-alveolar sibilant) is a fricative that is articulated with the tongue in a hollow shape, usually with the tip of the tongue (apex) against the alveolar ridge. It is a sibilant sound and is found most notably in a number of languages in a linguistic area covering northern and central Iberia. It is most well known from its occurrence in the Spanish of this area. In the Middle Ages, it occurred in a wider area, covering Romance languages spoken throughout France, Portugal, and Spain, as well as Old High German and Middle High German."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant]

ConsPhnm./c/-[ts]

description::
Â· example: tsunami,
Â· Greek: Ï„Ïƒ,
Â· pinyin: z,
"A voiceless alveolar affricate is a type of affricate consonant pronounced with the tip or blade of the tongue against the alveolar ridge (gum line) just behind the teeth. This refers to a class of sounds, not a single sound. There are several types with significant perceptual differences:
The voiceless alveolar sibilant affricate [tÍ¡s] is the most common type and has an abrupt hissing sound, as the ts in English cats.
The voiceless alveolar retracted sibilant affricate [tÍ¡sÌº], also called apico-alveolar or grave, has a weak hushing sound reminiscent of retroflex affricates. It is found e.g. in Basque, where it contrasts with a more conventional non-retracted laminal alveolar affricate.
The voiceless alveolar non-sibilant affricate [tÍ¡Î¸Ì ] or [tÍ¡Î¸Í‡], using the alveolar diacritic from the Extended IPA, is somewhat similar to the th in some pronunciations of English eighth. It is found as a regional realization of the sequence /tr/ in some Sicilian dialects of Standard Italian.
The voiceless alveolar lateral affricate [tÍ¡É¬] is found in certain languages, such as Cherokee, Icelandic and Nahuatl."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_affricate]

ConsPhnm./k/

description::
Â· example: kit,
Â· pinyin: g,
"The voiceless velar stop or voiceless velar plosive is a type of consonantal sound used in almost all spoken languages. The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents this sound is âŸ¨kâŸ©, and the equivalent X-SAMPA symbol is k.
The [k] sound is a very common sound cross-linguistically. Most languages have at least a plain [k], and some distinguish more than one variety. Most Indo-Aryan languages, such as Hindi and Bengali, have a two-way contrast between aspirated and plain [k]. Only a few languages lack a voiceless velar stop, e.g. Tahitian.
Some languages have the voiceless pre-velar stop,[1] which is articulated slightly more front compared with the place of articulation of the prototypical voiceless velar stop, though not as front as the prototypical voiceless palatal stop.
Conversely, some languages have the voiceless post-velar stop,[2] which is articulated slightly behind the place of articulation of the prototypical voiceless velar stop, though not as back as the prototypical voiceless uvular stop."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voiceless_velar_stop]

ConsPhnm./h/

description::
Â· example: hear,
Â· Greek: Ï‡,
"The voiceless glottal fricative, sometimes called voiceless glottal transition, and sometimes called the aspirate,[1][2] is a type of sound used in some spoken languages that patterns like a fricative or approximant consonant phonologically, but often lacks the usual phonetic characteristics of a consonant. The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents this sound is âŸ¨hâŸ©, and the equivalent X-SAMPA symbol is h, although [h] has been described as a voiceless vowel because in many languages, it lacks the place and manner of articulation of a prototypical consonant as well as the height and backness of a prototypical vowel:
[h and É¦] have been described as voiceless or breathy voiced counterparts of the vowels that follow them [but] the shape of the vocal tract [â€¦] is often simply that of the surrounding sounds. [â€¦] Accordingly, in such cases it is more appropriate to regard h and É¦ as segments that have only a laryngeal specification, and are unmarked for all other features. There are other languages [such as Hebrew and Arabic] which show a more definite displacement of the formant frequencies for h, suggesting it has a [glottal] constriction associated with its production.[3]
LamÃ© contrasts voiceless and voiced glottal fricatives.[4]"
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative]

description::

Â· Greek: Î³,
Â· char.121'y' char.947'Î³'
"The voiced velar fricative is a type of consonantal sound, used in various spoken languages. It is not found in Modern English but it existed in Old English {Baker, 2012, P. 15}.[full citation needed] The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents this sound is âŸ¨É£âŸ©, a Latinized variant of the Greek letter gamma, âŸ¨Î³âŸ©, which has this sound in Modern Greek. It should not be confused with the graphically similar âŸ¨É¤âŸ©, the IPA symbol for a close-mid back unrounded vowel, which some writings[1] use for the voiced velar fricative.
The symbol âŸ¨É£âŸ© is also sometimes used to represent the velar approximant, though that is more accurately written with the lowering diacritic: [É£Ì] or [É£Ë•]. The IPA also provides a dedicated symbol for a velar approximant, [É°], though there can be stylistic reasons to not use it in phonetic transcription.
There is also a voiced post-velar fricative (also called pre-uvular) in some languages. For voiced pre-velar fricative (also called post-palatal), see voiced palatal fricative."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voiced_velar_fricative]

ConsPhnm./r/

description::
Â· example: ring,
Â· Greek: Ï,
"The alveolar approximant is a type of consonantal sound used in some spoken languages. The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents the alveolar and postalveolar approximants is âŸ¨É¹âŸ©, a lowercase letter r rotated 180 degrees. The equivalent X-SAMPA symbol is r\.
There is no separate symbol for the dental approximant (as in Spanish nada) in the International Phonetic Alphabet, which most scholars transcribe with the symbol for a voiced dental fricative, âŸ¨Ã°âŸ©.
The most common sound represented by the letter r in English is the postalveolar approximant, pronounced a little more back and transcribed more precisely in IPA as âŸ¨É¹Ì âŸ©, but âŸ¨É¹âŸ© is often used for convenience in its place. For further ease of typesetting, English phonemic transcriptions might use the symbol âŸ¨râŸ© even though this symbol represents the alveolar trill in phonetic transcription."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alveolar_and_postalveolar_approximants]

ConsPhnm./l/

description::
Â· example: love,
Â· Greek: Î»,
"The alveolar lateral approximant is a type of consonantal sound used in many spoken languages. The symbol in the International Phonetic Alphabet that represents dental, alveolar, and postalveolar lateral approximants is âŸ¨lâŸ©, and the equivalent X-SAMPA symbol is l.
As a sonorant, lateral approximants are nearly always voiced. Voiceless lateral approximants, /lÌ¥/ are common in Sino-Tibetan languages, but uncommon elsewhere. In such cases, voicing typically starts about halfway through the hold of the consonant. No language is known to contrast such a sound with a voiceless alveolar lateral fricative [É¬].
In a number of languages, including most varieties of English, the phoneme /l/ becomes velarized ("dark l") in certain contexts. By contrast, the non-velarized form is the "clear l" (also known as: "light l"), which occurs before and between vowels in certain English standards.[1] Some languages have only clear l.[2] Others may not have a clear l at all, or only before front vowels (especially [i])."
[{2019-08-11} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_lateral_approximants]

"The voiceless palato-alveolar sibilant affricate or voiceless domed postalveolar sibilant affricate is a type of consonantal sound used in some spoken languages. The sound is transcribed in the International Phonetic Alphabet with âŸ¨tÍ¡ÊƒâŸ©, âŸ¨tÍœÊƒâŸ© or âŸ¨tÊƒâŸ© (formerly the ligature âŸ¨Ê§âŸ©). The alternative commonly used in American tradition is âŸ¨ÄâŸ©. It is familiar to English speakers as the "ch" sound in "chip".
Historically, this sound often derives from a former voiceless velar stop /k/ (as in English church; also in Gulf Arabic, Slavic languages, Indo-Iranian languages and Romance languages), or a voiceless dental stop /t/ by way of palatalization, especially next to a front vowel (as in English nature; also in Amharic, Portuguese etc.)."

stress of wordLogHmn

description::
"Accent: There are three kinds of accent in world languages: stress accents, pitch accents, and tones. Here accent doesn't mean different varieties of pronunciation, such as in "His English has a Texan accent," but it means a way to distinguish words other than consonants and vowels.
English has stress accents, where the strong voice determines accents. For instance, the words subject in "the subject" and in "to subject" have different accents while they have the same consonants and vowels.
Japanese has pitch accents, where the high tone of voice determines accents. The strength of voice doesn't matter in Japanese to differentiate words.
Chinese has tones, where every syllable has either one of the four tones. Unlike Chinese, wrong accents don't make much trouble in Japanese, so you can skip this section if you want to master kana first."
[http://www.sf.airnet.ne.jp/ts/japanese/phoneme.html]

EVOLUTING of SpchLago

description::
"The evolution of oral languages is a complex process spanning tens of thousands of years, shaped by biological, cultural, and social factors. Below is a concise overview of how oral languages likely developed and evolved, based on current understanding:
### 1. **Origins of Language (Prehistoric Roots)**
- **Biological Foundations**: The capacity for language is tied to human evolution, particularly changes in the brain and vocal tract. Around 100,000â€“50,000 years ago, *Homo sapiens* developed the anatomical ability (e.g., a descended larynx) and cognitive capacity (e.g., complex neural networks) for articulate speech. This distinguishes humans from other primates, whose vocalizations are less flexible.
- **Proto-Languages**: Early humans likely used proto-linguistic systemsâ€”combinations of gestures, grunts, and simple vocalizationsâ€”to communicate basic needs (e.g., hunting, social bonding). These systems were not full languages but laid the groundwork.
- **Symbolic Communication**: The shift to symbolic thought, evidenced by cave art and tools (~40,000 years ago), suggests humans began associating sounds with abstract concepts, a key step toward language.
### 2. **Development of Early Languages**
- **Divergence and Migration**: As human populations spread across Africa, Europe, Asia, and beyond (~70,000â€“50,000 years ago), isolated groups developed distinct vocal systems. Linguistic diversity emerged as communities adapted sounds and structures to their environments and needs.
- **Sound Systems**: Early languages likely had simple phonemic inventories (e.g., fewer consonants and vowels) and basic grammar. Over time, these grew more complex as social structures and cultural needs expanded.
- **Oral Tradition**: Without writing, early languages relied on oral transmission, with storytelling, songs, and rituals shaping vocabulary and grammar. Mnemonic devices like repetition and rhythm helped preserve knowledge.
### 3. **Language Families and Diversification**
- **Language Families**: By 10,000â€“5,000 years ago, distinct language families (e.g., Indo-European, Sino-Tibetan, Niger-Congo) emerged. These are groups of languages sharing a common ancestor, reconstructed through comparative linguistics. For example, Proto-Indo-European, spoken ~6,000 years ago, gave rise to languages like Latin, Sanskrit, and Germanic.
- **Cultural and Technological Influence**: Agriculture (~10,000 BCE) and trade fostered contact between groups, leading to borrowing of words and grammatical structures. Meanwhile, isolation preserved unique languages.
- **Sound Shifts**: Systematic changes in pronunciation (e.g., Grimmâ€™s Law in Germanic languages) drove divergence within language families, creating new dialects and languages.
### 4. **Impact of Writing and Civilization**
- **Writing Systems (~3,000 BCE)**: The invention of writing (e.g., cuneiform, hieroglyphs) began to standardize some languages, though oral traditions remained dominant. Writing preserved languages and allowed for more complex administration and literature.
- **Standardization and Dialects**: As empires (e.g., Roman, Chinese) expanded, dominant languages like Latin or Mandarin spread, often suppressing local tongues. Dialects emerged in peripheral regions, some evolving into distinct languages (e.g., Romance languages from Latin).
- **Language Contact**: Trade, conquest, and migration led to pidgins and creolesâ€”simplified languages blending elements from multiple sources. For example, Swahili emerged from Bantu and Arabic influences.
### 5. **Modern Era and Globalization**
- **Colonialism and Language Spread**: From the 15th century, European colonization spread languages like English, Spanish, and French globally, often displacing indigenous languages. This reduced linguistic diversity but enriched vocabularies through borrowing.
- **Standardization and Education**: National education systems and media (19thâ€“20th centuries) standardized languages, reducing dialect variation. For example, Standard Mandarin became dominant in China.
- **Globalization and English**: English emerged as a global lingua franca due to economic, technological, and cultural dominance, influencing other languages through loanwords and syntax.
### 6. **Current Trends and Future Directions**
- **Language Endangerment**: Of the ~7,000 languages spoken today, half may vanish by 2100 due to globalization and cultural assimilation. Efforts like language revitalization aim to preserve endangered tongues (e.g., Navajo, Welsh).
- **Digital Influence**: Technology (e.g., social media, AI) introduces new vocabulary and communication styles, like internet slang or emojis. Machine translation also reduces barriers between languages.
- **Language Evolution Continues**: Languages adapt to new contexts, with shifts in pronunciation, grammar, and vocabulary driven by social changes and contact. For example, Spanglish blends English and Spanish in bilingual communities.
### Key Mechanisms of Language Evolution
- **Phonological Change**: Sounds shift over time (e.g., the Great Vowel Shift in English, ~1400â€“1700 CE).
- **Lexical Change**: Words are borrowed, invented, or lost (e.g., "internet" or obsolete terms like "thou").
- **Grammatical Change**: Syntax and morphology simplify or complexify (e.g., Old English had complex cases; Modern English is simpler).
- **Social Factors**: Migration, trade, conquest, and technology drive language contact and change.
If youâ€™d like, I can dive deeper into a specific language, family, or mechanism of change, or analyze relevant sources for more detail. Let me know!"

SpchLago.tonal

description::
Ã— Mcsh-creation: {2025-07-18},
"Tonal languagesâ€”those in which pitch differences distinguish lexical or grammatical meaningâ€”have arisen independently in many parts of the world through a variety of phonetic and phonological processes. This overview traces the primary mechanisms of tonogenesis (toneâ€‘creation), the major areal developments in Southeast Asia, Africa, and the Americas, and the subsequent evolution of complex tone systems.
**What is tonogenesis?**
Tonogenesis is the process by which distinctive tonal contrasts develop from earlier nonâ€‘tonal features, most commonly through the reanalysis of laryngeal or voicing distinctions on consonants as pitch differences on adjacent vowels ([Wikipedia][1]).
---
## 1. Phonetic sources of tone
1. **Laryngeal and voicing contrasts**
* In many languages, the loss or weakening of voicing distinctions on consonants (e.g. between *b* vs. *p*) leaves residual differences in pitch onset on the following vowel, which over time can become phonologized as tone.
* Voiced consonants often induce a lower voiceâ€”or breathy voiceâ€”on the following vowel, while voiceless aspirated consonants induce a higher pitch ([Frontiers][2], [ResearchGate][3]).
2. **Final consonant loss**
* In Chinese, the four tones of Middle Chinese (å¹³â€¯pÃ­ng, ä¸Šâ€¯shÇng, å»â€¯qÃ¹, å…¥â€¯rÃ¹) are each reflexes of different lost final consonants in Old Chinese. For instance, words that ended in *-Ê”* developed a rising tone, while those ending in *-s* developed a departing tone ([Wikipedia][4]).
3. **Vowel quality and duration**
* Although less common, differences in vowel length or quality can also provide the phonetic groundwork for tone split or merger. These tend to play a secondary role compared to consonantâ€‘induced effects ([haskinslabs.org][5]).
---
## 2. Major areal developments
### Southeast Asia
* **Sinoâ€‘Tibetan** (e.g. Chinese): Protoâ€‘Sinoâ€‘Tibetan was likely nonâ€‘tonal; tone developed in Early Middle Chinese via final consonant loss and laryngeal splits, then spread areally to neighboring Tibetoâ€‘Burman and Vietic languages ([PubMed][6], [Wikipedia][4]).
* **Austroasiatic** (e.g. Vietnamese): Vietnamese tonogenesis mirrors Chinese patternsâ€”contrastive voicing on initials conditioned pitch on vowels, and later the loss of final glottal stops and *-s* created additional contour tones ([Oxford Research Encyclopedias][7], [Reddit][8]).
### Africa
* **Nigerâ€“Congo** (e.g. Bantu): Protoâ€‘Bantu had two level tones (High, Low); regular sound changes such as Meeussenâ€™s rule (flattening HHH to HLL, etc.) gradually conditioned more complex toneâ€‘accent systems in daughter languages ([Wikipedia][9]).
* **Mande and Voltaâ€‘Congo**: Independent tonogenesis via similar voicing-induced pitch splits demonstrates convergent evolution within different branches of Nigerâ€“Congo ([Frontiers][2]).
### The Americas
* **Otoâ€‘Manguean** (Mexico): Among the worldâ€™s oldest continuously tonal families, tone here likely arose from laryngeal consonant conditioning and register splits very early, giving rise to up to ten contrastive tones in some languages ([Wikipedia][10]).
* **Mixeâ€‘Zoque and Otomanguean interactions**: Areal diffusion in Mesoamerica spread tonal patterns beyond a single family.
---
## 3. From simple to complex tone systems
Once tone is established, languages can undergo:
1. **Tone splits and mergers**â€”e.g. a single tone may split into two (register split) conditioned by voicing of the following consonant, doubling the inventory.
2. **Contour development**â€”level tones may develop rising or falling contours through further phonetic drift (e.g. Vietnameseâ€™s sixâ€‘tone system).
3. **Downstep and terracing**â€”tonal downstep (a lowered high tone) can become phonemic, adding a layer of complexity beyond level vs. contour distinctions ([ResearchGate][3], [Taylor & Francis Online][11]).
---
## 4. Phylogenetic and areal perspectives
Recent phylogenetic analyses confirm that tone presence has strong familyâ€‘level patterns (e.g. Protoâ€‘Sinoâ€‘Tibetan was nonâ€‘tonal) but also highlight multiple independent origins. Tonogenesis remains an active area of study, linking historical linguistics with phonetics and cognitive science ([PMC][12], [PubMed][6]).
---
**In summary**, tonal systems have repeatedly evolved through the reanalysis of consonantal voice and laryngeal features, with convergent developments in Southeast Asia, Africa, and the Americas. Once established, tones can proliferate through splits, contour formation, and downstep, resulting in the rich typological diversity observed today.
